I have to say, I ... would need to know more about what Bo was actually doing to make this a problem before I could agree that things are as "bad" as presented. I kind-of agree with "Karen," here: it's just pretend. Now, we have your side of the story, telling us that she went off on a random rant about second amendment rights, but unfortunately, I have seen enough of these kinds of altercations to have to reserve judgment. It sounds pretty extreme to go off like that, and there are fools who will do so, but there are also perfectly reasonable people who will get portrayed as such fools when you only hear the other side of the story.  ...that said, the 180 shift when Ms. Sherriff comes in is absolutely a sign that she's a disingenuous twit. >_<  And, frankly, if it's JUST that he's playing "guns" and "bad guy," that doesn't sound like a problem to me. If he's actually disrupting other kids' games (as is implied, but not explicitly stated, in the story), then THAT behavior needs to be curtailed. But by focusing on the "gun" aspect of the imaginary play, you stigmatize "guns" as the sole problem, leaving the actual bad behavior as a-ok if he can figure out a way to do that without guns.  You want Bo to stop pointing guns at his classmates? Get him gun safety training. I guarantee you that the grown-ups teaching such classes will instill trigger discipline and the proper respect for firearms that he is not showing. And it will be far more effective than "guns are bad, and children can't know anything about them" taboo-training.    All of that said? When I was a kid, my dinosaurs flipped over to become laser guns all the time. My triceratops's horns could generate force fields to block them. I built space ships out of blocks that had all sorts of weapons, and the cylindrical blocks were hand-held phasers (I was a trek nerd even back then). And I don't even own a gun, today. They're more trouble to maintain and practice with than they're worth to me, as I thankfully don't live in a dangerous area. I probably _should_ train with at least a handgun and get a concealed carry permit, but I just can't be bothered.  So, no, playing with pretend guns as a kid is not a dangerous thing. At all.  Disrupting other kids' playtime by taking it over and ruining their games IS bad behavior, however, and should be stopped.

Anthropogenic global warming isn't a myth; it's a hoax. The climate all over the planet is changing all the time, has been since long, long before the industrial revolution, has been since long before we think anthro- existed on the planet to create anthropogenic _anything._ And actual science - as opposed to carefully cherry-picked data that uses different methods RIGHT AROUND when it shows changes happening - demonstrates that it has not accelerated nor slowed down since the start of the industrial era.   Moreover, the last warming period led to enormous prosperity in Europe and Asia (we have no records for the Americas or Africa) as farmland became more prosperous and there were even vinyards in northern England.  So, if you believe that man is causing the world to end, you, too, are a victim of lies.   We can and should be careful about litter, and cleaning up pollution is a good thing. But it isn't the crisis that it's made out to be, and we certainly shouldn't be curtailing human lives and advancement to accommodate unrealistic and unnecessary eco-goals. They generally lead to MORE environmental destruction, anyway.

There is nothing wrong with the boy playing with a block like its a gun, the kid probably just enjoys action adventure media or hunts at home with his family. I grew up in a family that both hunted and handled guns quite often, if taught proper safety at a young age (for me it was around this boy's age if not a year younger) pretend gun games are completely harmless. Hell me and my little brother would actively carve spears and bows using out bush craft sets (machetes, hatchets, and other assorted tools) then run around the woods shooting or spearing trees acting like they were either extinct animals or any other various fun things we wanted to hunt. We had everything from pellet rifles to BB guns (there is a difference but i won't get into that here) and we used to play with them just the same, though we were taught never to point them at anyone and even a toy gun can bring out bad situations (someone mistaking it for a real gun and you getting hurt or something akin to that) the boy should have just been reprimanded for pointing at people who were not engaged in the game with him, but those who were playing with him and knew it was just a game would have faced no more danger from a toy block than a tag in hide and seek. Its just kids being kids and having interests, one shouldn't punish or shame someone for having interests like this, i remember being in the kid's shoes when i drew hunters or warriors with their guns and kills (successful hunts, battle images, ect i loved creating stories that were more than just simple fables as i watched a lot of monster horror and action films growing up), teachers have called me psycho, weird, and every other word under the sun because i grew up with a family that hunted for their food and understood both the dangers and usefulness guns had and enjoyed using these tools in my art or games I'd play. While i sympathize with you dealing with a Karen I don't entirely agree with you punishing the boy for just having a healthy interest in something that's very real, he needed guidance not stifling or punishment.

I do have to say that even in the South, we need to discourage kids from playing guns not because of any anti-gun mentality, but merely because kids don't understand context and need to learn that anything qualifying as "school," including childcare facilities, are a no-gun zone. This reminds me of the time when I was 6 and one of the younger kids swore to me their uncle killed someone with a finger gun....like, they were convinced that they just did the finger gun thing and killed someone with their bare hands by shooting them with their bare hands. Even at the age of 6 I knew that wasn't possible. That a real gun had to be involved or some other weapon or no one actually even died, one of those, cause you can't shoot someone with your hand. Humans can't do that. You can strangle them, you can punch them, but you can't shoot them. Do I agree that pretend guns are bad? No. But be responsible. Teach your children reality from fiction. And definitely teach them to use their brains to know when it is ok and when it is not. At home with your sibling or parent who enjoys it too? Sure. At a public place where you disrupting others who are not into it? No. As long as you aren't disrupting or hurting anyone, I don't care. But if you've been told repeatedly that it is not ok somewhere, then follow that. You won't die from not getting to pretend to shoot things.  That all said, I much preferred pretend swords over guns as a kid and I got a scar to prove it. (Moral of that is, don't use metal poles as swords. Something I will surely tell my future children that if they wanna have a sword fight, they better be smarter than I was about it.)

I'd also have to chop wood with my siblings and I don't want to seem full of myself..- or anything, but I was pretty good with an axe. After that me and one of my friends, who was the closest person to the kid with the ball pump, ran to the school building that we weren't able to get into before because there's a gate that the after school teacher just opened.
